    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="f08d08f546a241108c99301c59a3c9e2">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'cfafd155-7646-477f-b762-55e6dbbda312'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

        <div class="relative"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" data-db-switch="icon-close_thin" aria-haspopup="true" aria-controls="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" role="button" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" aria-label="Tools" class="article-tools__ctrl"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Tools"></i><span>Tools</span></a><div data-db-target-of="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" aria-labelledby="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" class="article-tools__block fixed dropBlock__holder"><ul role="menu" class="rlist w-slide--list"><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="https://advanced.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/action/rightsLink?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202418322&amp;mode=" role="menuitem" target="_blank"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-permissions-key"></i><span>Request permission</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202418322" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-export-icon"></i><span>Export citation</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/personalize/addFavoritePublication?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202418322" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-favorites"></i><span>Add to favorites</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/addCitationAlert?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202418322" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Track-citations"></i><span>Track citation</span></a></li></ul></div></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d14036136" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>P2-type layered transition metal oxides are potential cathodes for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), but they commonly suffer from severe capacity degradation owing to multiple phase transitions and Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering during the extraction/insertion process. An anionic/cationic co-doping strategy at high sodium contents is proposed to effectively achieve high-rate and long-term stability of P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. The resulting Na<sub>0.75</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>1.95</sub>F<sub>0.05</sub> (NMNMOF) cathode delivers a reversible capacity of 116 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 75&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and maintains an initial capacity of 73% at 1500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles. The Mg/F anionic/cationic co-doping strategy impacts the local environment of the surrounding transition metal and oxygen, regulates the electron distribution, and modifies the initial diffusion state of Na sites, enhancing the diffusion ability of Na<sup>+</sup>. Moreover, the phase transition of P2-O2 is well suppressed and the decrease in Mn<sup>3+</sup> content greatly alleviates the Jahn–Teller effect to enhance structural stability. The full-cell devices with NMNMOF cathode and hard carbon anode demonstrate a high capacity of 80 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C and an excellent cycle life of over 500 cycles for applications. The anionic/cationic co-doping strategy will inspire the rational design of P2-type layered oxides and provide a new perspective for advanced SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202418322-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202418322-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Rechargeable battery technology has moved forward in recent years due to the rising demand for renewable energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0001_R_d14036128e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0003_R_d14036128e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To satisfy the demands of large-scale energy storage systems, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are an essential component of rechargeable batteries and a potential candidate for supplementing lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0004_R_d14036128e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Science">4</a>-<a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0009_R_d14036128e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Power Sources">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Positive electrode materials serve as a critical part of batteries, significantly impact the battery's energy density, and exert a vital effect on the battery. However, SIBs have yet to reach the commercial standards for widespread use since there is a lack of appropriate positive electrode materials available. Among numerous categories of positive electrode materials, layered oxide Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> is regarded as the most promising cathode material for commercialization on account of its large theoretical capacity.</p>
         
         <p>P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, one of many layered oxides, has a large theoretical capacity because of the two electrons that can be transferred.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0010_R_d14036128e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Storage Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0011_R_d14036128e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Sustain. Chem. Eng.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, there are still many challenges in its actual application. Phase transition is one of the factors that seriously degrades its electrochemical performance. The electrostatic shielding effect between layers is weakened by the deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>, which can result in relative slip between plates and hazardous phase transitions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0012_R_d14036128e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Storage Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0013_R_d14036128e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Phys. B+C">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, when Na<sup>+</sup> is released, the phenomenon of Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering frequently happens, which is brought on by the interaction of charge ordering linked to the transition metal potential difference and Na<sup>+</sup>. The ultimate superlattice structure will significantly restrict the material's rate and cycle performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0014_R_d14036128e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Sci. Adv.">14</a>-<a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0016_R_d14036128e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the spontaneously generated Jahn–Teller effect can cause severe volume changes and structural distortion of octahedra, which can readily result in migration and dissolution of transition metals as well as Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordered.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0017_R_d14036128e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Science">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The consequences of migration are voltage hysteresis and lower reversible capacity, while dissolution will cause transition metals to migrate to the negative side and deposit on the anode side. This not only thickens the SEI film and decreases the active Na<sup>+</sup>, but also increases the internal resistance of the battery and accelerates the decomposition of the electrolyte, significantly shortening the battery's cycle life.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0018_R_d14036128e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Energy">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0019_R_d14036128e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In recent years, the aforementioned problems of P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> have been effectively improved by a variety of techniques, mainly including element doping,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0020_R_d14036128e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Nano">20</a>-<a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0022_R_d14036128e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> surface coating,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0023_R_d14036128e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Front. Energy Res.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0024_R_d14036128e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and micro/nanoengineering.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0025_R_d14036128e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Batter. Supercaps">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, doping with heteroatoms has been comprehensively studied as the most effective method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0026_R_d14036128e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nano Lett.">26</a>-<a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0031_R_d14036128e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Lett.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Heteroatoms doped at different sites frequently have distinct functions. For instance, Peng et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0032_R_d14036128e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> designed [Na<sub>0.67</sub>Zn<sub>0.05</sub>] Ni<sub>0.18</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> by introducing Zn into alkali metal sites and Cu into transition metal sites, which maintained a capacity of 80.6% after 2000 cycles at a high rate of 10 C. Liu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0033_R_d14036128e593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> confirmed that Mg doping at transition metal sites in Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mg<sub>0.08</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> can reinforce the TMO<sub>2</sub> layer, alleviate severe phase transitions, and enhance structural stability. Moreover, F doping has also been reported to change interlayer spacing and reduce the average valence state of transition metal ions, enhancing the stability of the TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedral structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0034_R_d14036128e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Mater. Chem. A">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is essential to select the right doping agent because there is a wide range of element selection and combinations. In light of this, we construct P2-type cathode materials rationally using a method of the synergistic effect of cations/anions on the matrix material.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, a high sodium content P2-type layered oxide Na<sub>0.75</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>1.95</sub>F<sub>0.05</sub> (denoted as NMNMOF) is prepared using Mg and F co-doping to create a promising layered cathode. This unusual-doped cathode could deliver high-capacity retentions of ≈100%, 75%, and 73% at 150, 500, and 1500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> over 100, 500, and 1000 cycles, respectively. Experimental and theoretical calculations confirmed that the synergistic effect between Mg and F helps to improve the electrochemical performance of SIBs. The results show that Mg/F doping improves the structural stability by limiting the Jahn–Teller effect of Mn and suppressing phase transition. In addition, it is also the fact that F doping stabilizes the lattice oxygen by turning the surrounding O<sup>2−</sup> into a new negative center and altering the charge distribution around it, retaining a certain limit of oxygen redox reactions while also stabilizing most of the lattice oxygen. This increases the capacity marginally while enhancing its cycling stability. More significantly, the charge center adjusts the initial distribution state of Na<sup>+</sup>, allowing more Na<sup>+</sup> at Na<sub>f</sub> sites to move to Na<sub>e</sub> sites and enhancing the rate performance of the battery. To summarize, the anion/cation co-doping strategy has the ability to suppress irreversible phase transitions, alleviate the Jahn–Teller effect and adjust the Na migration, which benefits greatly to the specific capacity, rate performance, and cycling stability of SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202418322-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202418322-sec-0020-title">2 Result and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202418322-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202418322-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structural and Morphological Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The designed product P2-NMNMOF was synthesized by a simple and rapid sol-gel method. The stoichiometric ratios of metal acetates are dissolved in deionized water with a small amount of citric acid added as a chelating agent. A series of final products are obtained following solvent evaporation and heat treatment at high temperatures for annealing, and the details are shown in the Experimental Section in <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">Supporting Information</a>. In addition, the control samples of Na<sub>0.75</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> are denoted as NMNMO and NNMO, respectively, which are prepared by the similar method as NMNMOF. The stoichiometry of NMNMOF is analyzed by the inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES). Specifically, the content of F<sup>−</sup> is measured by using ion chromatography, and the results have good consistency with the design system (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The crystal structures of these three samples are characterized using powder X-ray diffraction (XRD), as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). The three samples are easily indexed as hexagonal structures (JCPDS-27-0751), which belong to the P2 type stacking and P63/mcm space group, with NMNMOF being a pure phase devoid of any additional impurities generated. Moreover, Rietveld refinement is carried out to obtain a better understanding of the structural information of the three samples, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0001">1a,b</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2–S4</a> (Supporting Information). The results demonstrate that in comparison to NNMO (a = 2.8713 Å, c = 11.1109 Å), the lattice parameters of NMNMOF (a = 2.8755 Å and c = 11.1198 Å) exhibit an expanded a–b-plane and an extended c-axis. More specifically, for NMNMOF, the interlayer distances of d<sub>(O−Na−O)</sub> and d<sub>(O−TM−O)</sub> are 3.4738 and 2.0861 Å, and 3.4518 and 2.0996 Å for NNMO, respectively, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0001">1c</a>. While NMNMOF exhibits a marginally reduced interlayer spacing between TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs, the enlarged NaO<sub>2</sub> slabs enable the triangular prism of the Na<sup>+</sup> transport channel to expand in space, which promotes better Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0035_R_d14036128e719" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The changes in crystal cell parameters are attributed to the introduction of Mg and F. More insight into the impact of F doping has been obtained through the refinement of NMNMO (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The interlayer distances of d<sub>(O−Na−O)</sub> and d<sub>(O−TM−O)</sub> are 3.4663 and 2.0815 Å. In contrast to NMNMOF, we can see that the distance between the NaO<sub>2</sub> layers increases slightly while the distance between TMO<sub>2</sub> decreases. In comparison to NMNMO, we can see that the distance between the NaO<sub>2</sub> layers further increases in NMNMOF, making Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion more favorable. This is due to the stronger electronegativity of F compared to O and smaller ionic radii, resulting in stronger covalent interactions with transition metals. Both of these factors work in concert to create structural alterations that are advantageous for the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>. It is worth noting that with the doping of dual sites, the content of Na<sub>e</sub> sites is gradually increasing, which is conducive to the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>. The specific details will be discussed in theoretical calculations. In addition, considering the possibility of Mg ions entering the Na layer, we place Mg ions at the Na sites for Rietveld refinement. The results show that the agreement factors of Rietveld refinement are significantly increased and contradict with the change in interlayer spacing, further confirming that Mg ions enter the TMO<sub>2</sub> layer (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202418322-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e7b81626-0d0a-4704-8365-6969fe1f927a/adfm202418322-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e7b81626-0d0a-4704-8365-6969fe1f927a/adfm202418322-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5aa5d74c-9e29-4658-b8d2-56c18f612911/adfm202418322-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e7b81626-0d0a-4704-8365-6969fe1f927a/adfm202418322-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202418322-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202418322" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Powder XRD profiles and Rietveld refinement patterns of (a) NNMO and (b) NMNMOF. (c) Schematic diagram of Mg and F incorporation induced structural variations. Characterization of structure and morphology of NMNMOF cathodes: (d) SEM and (e) TEM images. (f) HRTEM analysis (the internal illustration shows the SAED pattern). (g) EDS mapping results.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>According to the images from scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0001">1d,e</a>. NMNMOF has a typical plate-like shape with a smooth surface. Concurrently, the NNMO and NMNMO control samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) likewise show a plate-like morphology. As illustrated by Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0001">1f</a>, high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) analysis reveals distinct lattice fringes with interplanar spacings of 5.56 Å, which are associated with the (002) planes of the conventional P2-type layered structure. The internal illustration is the selected area electron diffraction pattern of the NMNMOF material, which further displays clear diffraction spots consistent with the hexagonal layered P2-type structure. Meanwhile, an analysis of the X-ray energy dispersive spectrum (EDS) shows that elements Na, Mg, Ni, Mn, O, and F exist, and elemental mapping results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0001">1g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) show that all contained elements are evenly distributed throughout the plate-like particle without aggregation.</p>
            
            <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) is used to confirm the chemical composition and valence states of the surface of NNMO and NMNMOF materials. The Mn 2p spectra are separated into four peaks at 641.74, 643.12, 653.19, and 653.39&nbsp;eV, with the peak at binding energy 641.74 and 653.19&nbsp;eV belonging to Mn<sup>3+</sup> and the other two peaks attributed to Mn<sup>4+</sup>, demonstrating the coexistence of two valence states of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> in NMMO. However, in NMNMOF, the Mn 2p spectrum contains only two peaks at 642.19 and 653.70&nbsp;eV, showing that the vast majority Mn<sup>4+</sup> valence state exists, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). To further confirm the valence state of Mn, we calculate the average oxidation state (AOS) of Mn using the formula AOS = 8.956–1.126 ∆E<sub>S</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0036_R_d14036128e811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Small">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> where ∆E<sub>S</sub> is disclosed to be 4.45&nbsp;eV by Mn 3 s spectrum, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information). Therefore, AOS is calculated to be 3.91, confirming that the majority of Mn has a valence state of +4. The doping of Mg and F diminishes the proportion of Mn<sup>3+</sup> and increases the proportion of Mn<sup>4+</sup>. Given that Mn<sup>3+</sup> is one of the primary triggers of the Jahn–Teller effect, this modification will effectively suppress the effect during the charging and discharging process. Furthermore, ex situ XPS measurement shows that the proportion of Mn<sup>4+</sup> increases in NMMO when charged to 4.3&nbsp;V, while the proportion of Mn<sup>3+</sup> elevates when discharged to 2.0&nbsp;V, indicating that Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> will compensate for the charge through redox reactions during the charging and discharging process of NMMO, thereby contributing to some of the capacity. However, Mn ions still maintain tetravalence during the charge/discharge process in NMNMOF, confirming that Mn only serves as an interlayer pillar. Similarly, the Ni 2p spectrum demonstrates that the redox reaction of Ni<sup>2+</sup>→Ni<sup>3+</sup>→Ni<sup>4+</sup> is responsible for the charge compensation. In addition, the proportion of Ni<sup>2+</sup> in NMNMOF contains more than that in NNMO, suggesting that anion doping and higher sodium content have raised the Ni<sup>2+</sup> ratio, as illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information). Moreover, Mg remains in a divalent state throughout the entire charge and discharge process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Therefore, one of the reasons for the poor cycling stability of NNMO is due to the structural damage caused by the Jahn–Teller distortion of some Mn<sup>3+</sup> ions. When using the strategy of Mg/F dual doping and high sodium content adjustment, the overall valence state of transition metals is diminished, especially Ni. However, Mn remains in a tetravalent state throughout the process, which is attributed to F mainly bonding with Mn, and the strong electronegativity of F weakens the ability of capturing electrons of Mn, specific analysis shown in the calculation section. Inspiringly, this strategy alleviates Jahn–Teller distortion, enhances structural stability, and thus achieves a long cycle life.</p>
            
            <p>The O 1s XPS spectra of NNMO and NMNMOF electrodes at different cut-off voltages during charge/discharge process are investigated to explore the anionic activity, as displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information) in the revised manuscript. Oxygen anionic redox is widely reported in many P2-type materials that oxygen can take part in the charge compensation process and contribute to extra capacity in the high-voltage window while being accompanied by severe structural destruction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0037_R_d14036128e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nat. Chem.">37</a>-<a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0039_R_d14036128e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Cent. Sci.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks at 529.7, 532.1, and 533.4&nbsp;eV responded to the lattice oxygen (O<sup>2−</sup>), charged peroxo-like oxygen (O<sup>n−</sup>, n&lt;2), and surface-deposited oxygen, respectively. The peaks at 531.3 and 533.1&nbsp;eV refer to the adsorbed CO<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O. The O 1s spectra at charge/discharge states show significant differences at 532.1&nbsp;eV when compared to the pristine, revealing a change in the oxygen valence state. The peak located at 537&nbsp;eV is attributed to electrolyte oxidation. When charged to 4.3&nbsp;V in the first cycle, the peak at 532.1&nbsp;eV corresponding to O<sup>n−</sup> emerges, indicating the presence of oxygen anionic redox in NMNMOF. However, the peak area representing O<sup>n−</sup> content remarkably attenuates when discharged to 2.0&nbsp;V, suggesting that the oxygen anionic redox can supply additional capacity in the discharge process. Notably, the ratio of 532.1&nbsp;eV peak in the modified NMNMOF is lower than that of NNMO when charged to 4.3&nbsp;V and the ratio of 529.7&nbsp;eV peak representing lattice oxygen peak is higher than that of NNMO when discharged to 2&nbsp;V, which means that the degree of oxygen anionic redox occurring in the high-voltage part decreases and most of the lattice oxygen is stabilized. This enhances structural stability while increasing the capacity of the electrode material slightly. In addition, we also study the behavior of Mg and F affecting the electronic structure of materials through DFT calculations and Bader charge analysis, the details are shown in the theoretical calculation section.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202418322-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202418322-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Properties</h3>
            
            <p>In order to evaluate the electrochemical performance of electrode materials, half cells are conducted by constant galvanostatic charge/discharge cycling. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0002">2a,b</a> shows the charge/discharge curves of NNMO and NMNMOF cathodes at 0.1 C as well as the 1st cycle and 100th cycle at 1 C within the voltage range of 2.0–4.3&nbsp;V. The curve of NNMO has multiple voltage platforms, and its long voltage platform at 4.2&nbsp;V represents the result of irreversible P2-O2 phase transition. The plateau's shape vanishes and the capacity decays dramatically after 100 cycles, suggesting that the material experiences significant structural alterations. However, the NMNMOF curve shows a shortened voltage plateau beyond 4.0&nbsp;V and remains virtually unchanged after 100 cycles with almost no capacity decay, which can be attributed to the restrained phase transition that has excellent structural stability. Specifically, after modification, the voltage plateau of Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering represented by 3.4 and 3.6&nbsp;V also becomes smoother, suggesting that this problem has also been alleviated to some extent and the migration of ions has been enhanced. In terms of rate performance, NMNMOF also exhibits impressive performance, providing reversible discharge capacities of 116, 113, 108, 99, and 80 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 75, 150, 250, 500, and 1500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0002">2c,d</a>. In addition, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0002">2e</a> demonstrates that the capacity retention rate is ≈100% and the capacity scarcely degrades over 100 cycles at 1 C. However, the capacity retention rate of NNMO is only 39% after 100 cycles at 1 C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The NMNMOF cathode has a high working voltage and delivers excellent energy density in half-cells compared to other reported P2-type layered cathodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0002">2f</a>). Furthermore, the comparing cycling performances of these three samples tested at 500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0002">2g</a>, respectively. Among them, the dual-site-doped NMNMOF shows the best cycling stability with capacity retention rates of 75% over 500 cycles, while NMNMO delivers a moderate performance, indicating the crucial role of Mg/F co-doping for achieving higher cyclability. This exhibits that the designed NMNMOF has the optimal cycling performance, which is closely related to alleviating irreversible phase transitions under high voltage. In addition, we also investigate the cycling performance of NMNMOF at higher rates, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0002">2h</a>. Under the condition of a current density of 10 C (1500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), the capacity retention rate of over 1000 cycles can reach 73%, demonstrating excellent cycling stability at high rates.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202418322-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e8f1551a-9ac0-4a72-b072-d5ab50e59685/adfm202418322-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>

            <div class="paragraph-element">To study the electrochemical behavior of the designed cathode material, the sodium ion diffusion coefficient is investigated by multisweep cyclic voltammetry (CV) tested at various scan rates (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0003">3a</a>). The NMNMOF shows an acceptable current response as the scan rate increases, while the NNMO exhibits a poor current response with severe peak current decay at a high scan rate (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). If there is no discernible change in <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8a5e2f65-e3dd-436f-8c28-48ef65a79298/adfm202418322-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202418322:adfm202418322-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202418322-math-0001.png">

            <p>However, the current response of NNMO rapidly decreases in the range exceeding 4&nbsp;V, indicating a strong P2-O2 phase transition, which seriously hinders the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>. On the contrary, NMNMOF exhibits good current responsiveness in the range above 4&nbsp;V, suggesting that the dual doping strategy prevents P2-O2 from going through an unfavorable phase transition and guarantees Na migration. It should be pointed out that a higher slope corresponds to a faster sodium transport rate. Compared to NNMO, NMNMOF appears to have a steeper and more linear slope during the charging and discharging processes, suggesting good Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0003">3b</a>). Moreover, NMNMOF shows stable <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/1979a5f0-6298-4501-b5b8-4224948dcf35/adfm202418322-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202418322:adfm202418322-math-0005" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202418322-math-0005.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>N</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>a</mi>
                              <mo>+</mo>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{D}_{N{{a}^ + }}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> of 6.99 × 10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, further indicating the fast kinetic. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12c</a> (Supporting Information), NMNMOF exhibits superior Na diffusion ability at each redox peak compared to NNMO, resulting in an overall high Na diffusion coefficient. Furthermore, NNMO presents complex peak changes during the charging process, while the curves of NMNMOF become smoother after dual doping, indicating better reversibility of phase transition. By comparing the CV of the first five cycles, it is found that NMNMOF has good coincidence, proving its good cycling performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). To gain a deeper understanding of the enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetics, Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests are carried out on the NNMO and NMNMOF electrodes to provide a more thorough comprehension of the improved Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetics.</p>
            
            <p>The GITT profiles of NNMO and NMNMOF electrodes during the charging process within the voltage range of 2.0–4.1&nbsp;V, based on which the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/0b463aec-f716-4e26-9299-6e80978b1372/adfm202418322-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202418322:adfm202418322-math-0006" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202418322-math-0006.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>N</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>a</mi>
                              <mo>+</mo>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{D}_{N{{a}^ + }}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> at different voltages are calculated,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0040_R_d14036128e1122" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">40</a>, <a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0041_R_d14036128e1122" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Nat. Chem.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0003">3c,d</a>. To avoid the influence of self-discharge and thermodynamic polarization in the high-voltage region on the calculation of diffusion coefficient, we only calculate based on the data in the low-voltage region. The diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> sharply drops during the plateau period of the NNMO electrode, while it undergoes consecutive changes during the charging process of the NMNMOF electrode. The <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/3c080c77-31d5-48b6-9027-f1d6e25215ef/adfm202418322-math-0007.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202418322:adfm202418322-math-0007" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202418322-math-0007.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>N</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>a</mi>
                              <mo>+</mo>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{D}_{N{{a}^ + }}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> of the NMNMOF electrode is greater than that of the NNMO electrode throughout the entire charging process. Visibly, the changed structure with the doping of Mg/F allows the electrode material to maintain a stable kinetic process throughout the entire electrochemical reaction process. The prominent Na<sup>+</sup> dynamics are more likely derived from the enlarged d-spacing and the preferred migration of Na<sub>e</sub>. Meanwhile, the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> in the high-pressure region can be more easily carried out due to the suppressed P2-O2 phase transition, this is also consistent with the analysis of CV results in the previous text. Overall, the kinetics of SIBs have been greatly improved from the dual doping method.</p>
            
            <p>The electrochemical behavior of NNMO, NMNMO, and NMNMOF electrodes is tested through electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) in a half cell. The R<sub>ct</sub> values of the NNMO, NMNMO, and NMNMOF electrode are calculated through fitting to be 521.7, 575.7, and 182.7 Ω, respectively, indicating that Mg/F dual doping can reduce the charge transfer resistance and accelerate the transfer kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>, which is attributed to the enlarged interlayer spacing. Furthermore, we test the EIS of electrodes with different numbers of cycles, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0003">3e,f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information) and the equivalent circuit is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information). Comparative analysis reveals that the R<sub>ct</sub> of NMNMOF is 299.3, 287, and 410.4 Ω after 5, 10, and 30 cycles, respectively. There is no significant increase when compared to NMMO and NMNMO (details in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), showing that the charge transfer process occurs more easily in the NMNMOF material system. In summary, the NMNMOF cathode has outstanding rate performance due to low charge transfer resistance and high charge transfer kinetics through the analysis of CV, GITT, and EIS.</p>
            
            <p>To further verify this unusual dual-site doping effect and explore the influence of changes in structure on electrochemical behavior during charge-discharge, in situ XRD measurement was performed and the findings are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0004">4</a>. It is worth noting that there are some peaks in XRD patterns created by the Be/BeO window, Al foil, separator, and cell pieces, as indicated by an asterisk and do not shift during the charge/discharge process. The figure shows the evolution process of XRD spectra of NNMO and NMNMOF during the first charging and discharging process. During the first charging process, the (002) and (004) peaks gradually move toward lower angles, whereas the (100) peak moves toward higher angles, showing that the lattice parameter c increases and the decrease of lattice parameter a during Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation. When charged above 4.2&nbsp;V in NNMO, a new peak at 20° related to the O2 structure appears (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0004">4a</a>). We find that a severe P2-O2 phase transition occurred in the high-pressure plateau region, which is consistent with the results reported in other works.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0032_R_d14036128e1186" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the discharge process, it cannot fully recover to the original state of pure P2 phase, indicating that its phase transition under high voltage is irreversible. There is extreme tension and further structural damage as a result of the lattice mismatch between the P2 and O2 structures. More significantly, the (004) peak splits when discharged to low voltage (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0004">4b</a>), which is attributed to structural degradation caused by the repeated generation of lattice distortion, Jahn–Teller distortion effect brought on by Mn<sup>3+</sup> and the in-plane Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0014_R_d14036128e1199" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Sci. Adv.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0042_R_d14036128e1199" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Carbon Neutraliz.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> thereby impairing electrochemical performance. However, there is no other impurity peaks appear when the charging exceeds 4.2&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0004">4d</a>), indicating that the P2-O2 phase transition prone to occur at high voltages is suppressed in NMNMOF to a great extent. We believe that the reason for the disappearance of P2-O2 phase transition is that more Na<sup>+</sup> stays in the NaO<sub>2</sub> layer to minimize the electrostatic repulsion of the O-O layer, making it less prone to occur relative sliding of the TMO<sub>2</sub> layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0043_R_d14036128e1214" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Energy Mater.">43</a>-<a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0047_R_d14036128e1214" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, it is worth noting that the (004) can progressively recover to its initial pure P2 phase state during the discharge process compared to the NNMO system and the peak changes are entirely reversible throughout the charging and discharging process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0004">4e</a>), suggesting that the suppressed P2-O2 phase transition under high voltage do not cause structural damage and still preserve structural stability. In addition, the Rietveld refinement is carried out to measure the variation of lattice parameters of NNMO and NMNMOF accurately (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0004">4c,f</a>). The parameter variations of NMNMOF exhibit 0.59% for ΔV which is smaller than the 1.8% variation of NNMO, indicating a stable structure enduring Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418322-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418322-bib-0047_R_d14036128e1228" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, the harmful P2-O2 phase transition is effectively mitigated under high voltage regions through the dual doping strategy of anions and cations, resulting in modest volume fluctuations and a stable structure, thus achieving a long cycle life for NMNMOF.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202418322-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6689b15d-3775-4c9c-a7ea-ef5eb3862c8b/adfm202418322-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6689b15d-3775-4c9c-a7ea-ef5eb3862c8b/adfm202418322-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6d867529-289b-4754-8fbb-683e6681c0cb/adfm202418322-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6689b15d-3775-4c9c-a7ea-ef5eb3862c8b/adfm202418322-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202418322-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202418322" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ X-ray diffraction analysis: (a) in situ XRD patterns of NNMO and (b) the corresponding intensity contour map. (c) corresponding variation of lattice parameters a, c, and V of NNMO. (d) in situ XRD patterns of NMNMOF and (e) the corresponding intensity contour map. (f) corresponding variation of lattice parameters a, c, and V of NMNMOF.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further reveal the structural stability of NNMO and NMNMOF, XRD and SEM characterization analyses are conducted on the cyclic products, as indicated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). Evidently, the XRD pattern of the NNMO electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a>, Supporting Information) after cycling 100 cycles at 1 C shows clear structural degradation with a few disappearing main peaks when compared with a pristine state. In contrast, the XRD pattern of the NMNMOF electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b</a>, Supporting Information) tested under the same condition shows no discernible change when compared with pristine material, suggesting that it has exceptional structural stability even after repeated charge/discharge process. Furthermore, the cycled NNMO electrode shows severe cracks that are detrimental to the electrochemical performance, according to SEM images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16c</a>, Supporting Information), while the NMNMOF electrode exhibits better morphology preservation due to enhanced structure complying with dual doping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16d</a>, Supporting Information). In short, the combined postcharacterization techniques illustrate that the NMNMOF cathode possesses prominent structural stability attributed to the Mg/F dual-site doping.</p>
            
            <p>Due to its exceptional rate performance, full cells based on the NMNMOF cathode and commercial hard carbon anode pretreated are assembled to investigate its potential uses, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0005">5a</a>. Based on the principle of charge balance in the full cell, we fit the mass ratio of the cathode and anode. Its charge and discharge curves at different current densities are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0005">5b</a>. It can provide reversible capacities of 115, 106, 96, 82, and 57 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 75, 150, 250, 500, and 1500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0005">5c</a>. Moreover, the full-cell exhibits similar charge/discharge curves with the half-cell, and an ≈106 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> capacity can be obtained in the voltage range of 2–4.3&nbsp;V at 1 C, while still maintaining 87% of the first cycle capacity after 100 cycles, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0005">5d</a>. The capacity retention rate is 73% after 500 cycles at a high rate of 10 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0005">5e</a>), demonstrating excellent stability, and rate performance, and has promising practical application prospects.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202418322-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9082ad81-e12a-4deb-a2bb-4e7d3fb154c4/adfm202418322-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9082ad81-e12a-4deb-a2bb-4e7d3fb154c4/adfm202418322-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bfca49cc-64b3-4bc0-9825-65b1ca0e7388/adfm202418322-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9082ad81-e12a-4deb-a2bb-4e7d3fb154c4/adfm202418322-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202418322-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202418322" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium-ion full-cell performances based on NMNMOF and commercial hard carbon: (a) Schematic illustration of the working principle in full-cell device. (b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of NMNMOF in different rates. (c) Rate properties of the full-cell device. (d,e) Cycling performance of the full cell at 1 C and 10 C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202418322-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202418322-sec-0050-title">2.3 Theoretical Calculation</h3>
            
            <p>To get a better understanding of the properties of Mg and F substituted structures, the electronic structure, charge density, and Na<sup>+</sup> migration barrier are investigated based on the constructed and optimized structures through density functional theory (DFT) calculations. First, Mn/Mn/Mg (configuration 1) and Mn/Mn/Ni (configuration 2) are constructed to more clearly identify the bonding mode of F. The formation energy results of calculation models indicate that the formation energy of configuration 1 is −4.86088&nbsp;eV, which is more negative than configuration 2, indicating a more stable configuration and F is more likely to bond with Mg, as illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0006">6a</a>. In light of the aforementioned findings, additional debate will be held. The electronic structures of NNMO and NMNMOF have been investigated to illustrate the effect on band structure after dual doping, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information). There is no bandgap found for spin-up states and spin-down states for both of them, indicating their metallic oxide feature. However, the difference after doping is that the distribution of electrons at the Fermi level increases, which is conducive to enhancing electronic conduction. To thoroughly investigate the redox behavior of O, we calculate the DOS under different states (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0006">6b</a>). At the initial state, the Ni band is near the Fermi level, and the oxidation process occurs by removing electrons from the Ni 3d orbit. When at a deep stage of sodium removal, the O 2p band approaches the Fermi level, indicating a small portion of lattice oxygen participates in the oxidation process and contributes a small amount of capacity. At the same time, partial hybridization of the Ni 3d band and the O 2p band allows the formation of bonding states of Ni-(O-O) through the charge transfer system (O to Ni charge transfer) to stabilize lattice oxygen, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0006">6c</a>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202418322-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0e356ef0-b212-4180-89e6-1afbbba68650/adfm202418322-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0e356ef0-b212-4180-89e6-1afbbba68650/adfm202418322-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/00a71428-c7c6-4e97-a26a-dd15e37037d5/adfm202418322-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0e356ef0-b212-4180-89e6-1afbbba68650/adfm202418322-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202418322-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202418322" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">DFT calculation: (a) Comparison of formation energy of two configurations. (b) total density of states (tDOS) and partial density of states (pDOS) of NMNMOF at different charge states (x = 0 and x = 0.5). (c) Schematic representation of the energy level versus DOS. Results of Bader charge analysis of (d) NMNMO and (e) NMNMOF (Green represents gaining electrons, blue represents losing electrons). (f) Calculation results of diffusion energy barrier of three samples. The overall schematic diagram of the diffusion path of (g) NNMO, (h) NMNMO, and (i) NMNMOF cathode.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The interactions between atoms are obtained by performing Bader charge analysis on various optimized structures, as indicated in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information). Notably, we have chosen atoms with the same position in various configurations for comparison to assure reliability. It is demonstrated by comparing the results of the NNMO and NMNMO that Mg doping increases the ability to obtain electrons at the O position where F will replace them, and also marginally increases the electronic ability of the vicinity of O, which results in more electrons enriched on them. This is due to the fact that magnesium has a lower electronegativity than nickel. However, it is discovered that the addition of F not only significantly decreases the electron acquisition ability at this site in comparison to NMNMO and NMNMOF but also increases the electron acquisition ability of the surrounding oxygen. Obtaining more electrons from surrounding oxygen is more conducive to stabilizing at −2 valence and is less prone to occur oxygen redox reactions, which is advantageous for the stability of lattice oxygen. This is also consistent with the DOS findings that the electron density of the O 2p orbital is lower than that of other orbitals, and E<sub>F</sub> is mainly contributed by the Mn orbital. We present the above results more concretely through charge density difference calculations, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0006">6d,e</a>.</p>
            
            <p>To study preliminarily the diffusion behavior on sodium-ion diffusion after dual-site doping, the diffusion energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> is calculated during the charging process, the results show that the migration energy barrier of the dual-doped sample is the lowest, which is 0.156&nbsp;eV lower than that of the undoped sample, significantly increasing the rate of Na<sup>+</sup> migration and advantageous for the rate performance of SIBs, as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0006">6f</a>. Most importantly, the diffusion path demonstrates that Na<sup>+</sup> moves from the center triangular prism's position to another triangular prism. Notably, comparing the migration paths of the three structures, it is discovered that the initial position of Na<sup>+</sup> have changed which first migrated from the Na<sub>f</sub> position to the Na<sub>e</sub> site prior to the subsequent migration in NMNMOF cathode, which is attributed to the effect of the doping of F, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information). The overall schematic diagram of the Na<sup>+</sup> migration path in three cathodes is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418322-fig-0006">6g–i</a>. The Na<sup>+</sup> at the Na<sub>f</sub> site is strongly repulsed by the transition metal layer, which hinders the migration of Na<sup>+</sup>. Therefore, the migration of the Na site causes the strong electrostatic repulsion from the transition metal layer to disappear, making the environment more favorable for Na migration. The migration of Na sites has also become one of the reasons why NMNMOF has good diffusion kinetics. The anionic/cationic dual-site doping technique can regulate the charge distribution around the atom, lower the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier, and adjust the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> sites, thus designing high-performance cathode materials.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202418322-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202418322-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we present a layered NMNMOF cathode material based on the NNMO matrix material through an anionic/cationic dual substitution strategy, which demonstrates superior rate capability and cycling stability. The synthesized Na<sub>0.75</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>1.95</sub>F<sub>0.05</sub> cathode can maintain a capacity retention rate of 73% after 1000 cycles at a high rate of 10 C. The structural stability has been regulated using the Mg/F co-doping technique, as evidenced by the strengthening of the TMO<sub>2</sub> layer and the significant suppression of the irreversible phase transition of P2-O2. Also, the rise in Na concentration and F doping decreases Mn<sup>3+</sup> content, thus inhibiting the Jahn–Teller effect and avoiding structural damage, while the oxygen redox reaction can be partially realized to contribute a small amount of capacity and decrease the loss of lattice oxygen. In addition, the diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> have been enhanced, reflected in the reduction of the diffusion energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> and the acceleration of its transportation. Notably, the diffusion pathway changes as Na<sup>+</sup> at the Na<sub>f</sub> site moves to the Na<sub>e</sub> site, improving the subsequent diffusion ability that follows. The decrease of Na<sub>f</sub> occupation weakens the resistance from the transition metal layer which is conducive to strengthening the ability to diffuse. This work has resolved the series of challenges Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> was facing to some extent through Mg/F dual-site doping, achieving high-rate and long cycle life of P2-type cathode materials. Besides, it clarifies the reason for the acceleration of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and also presents new perspectives and approaches for the future development of high-performance SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

      <section class="article-section article-section__references" id="article-references-section-1">

            <div class="accordion__content" style="display: none;" role="region" aria-labelledby="references-section-1" id="adfm202418322-bibl-0001">
               <ul class="rlist separator">
                  
                  <li data-bib-id="adfm202418322-bib-0001"><span class="bullet">1</span><span class="author">Z. Zhao</span>, <span class="author">K. Xia</span>, <span class="author">Y.
